B00CUIVGZS|5.0|Even though it's Chick's first release of all-new material in many years, I nearly passed when I saw the cover. A garish portrait depicting Chick clad in a suit of armor, lance in hand, riding a horse? And didn't we do the knight-on-a-horse thing before (Romantic Warrior) and make it look...a bit cooler? Can you maybe lower the mask?  Uh oh...<br /><br />I should know better by now. Corea's self-indulgent costuming on his album covers never told the listener anything about the quality of the music therein. I'm remembering his get-ups on the covers of The Leprechaun, My Spanish Heart, Secret Agent, the Mad Hatter ... all of which had silly covers and stellar music within. This is one of those albums. Ridiculous cover, seriously good music within. So Chick can wear whatever he wants, as long as the music delivers. And, good news, it sure does here.<br /><br />The first half of The Vigil contains predictably good music played by a great band. Recognizable Corea chord changes and arrangements. Solid back up players. For the most part, Vigil sounds like what you might expect; a classic Chick Corea release in a way that should please Corea fans and jazz fans alike. No big surprises, but very listenable music.<br /><br />If there was anything missing for me early on, it was a bit more fire. The truly exciting moments that make me want to hit replay. As the CD progresses, however, great moments start to accrue. \\"Portals to Forever\\" has excellent electric and acoustic piano passages, good interplay; a great electric solo by Hadrien Feraud.<br /><br />\\"Outside of Space\\" with Gayle Moran on vocals. What can I say? It reminded me a bit of the composition \\"Drifting\\" from Secret Agent (1978). Lovely piano, nice arrangement, but it doesn't save the song for me. But it's only 5 minutes.<br /><br />And then all's forgiven anyway with the final tracks, which make this a definite must-have CD.<br /><br />\\"Pledge for Peace,\\" featuring Ravi Coltrane evokes the spirit of later John Coltrane effectively in its intro and outro. Then it moves into a very spirited, aggressive acoustic number, with Corea giving a stellar performance, angular piano lines, exciting. Not to mention excellent support from the band.<br /><br />\\"Legacy,\\" is another standout, this time with Corea showing his chops on electric keyboards (in a way that is, to my ears, more exciting than I've heard in recent years). Stanley Clarke is on bass for this one. A very strong, loosely structured piece with a nice hook. It sort of recalled \\"Got a Match\\" in composition, but, for my taste, much more satisfying.<br /><br />A nice live version of a very hot \\"Hot House\\" (not part of CD configuration but I assume available as a download?) has great solos all around and completes The Vigil on a high note. It's in these last few tracks that I felt the fire I was seeking, and it is considerable heat.<br /><br />The Vigil is a generous CD. Those who remember vinyl may recall that 17 minutes took up nearly an entire album side. \\"Pledge for Peace\\" clocks in at 17 1/2 minutes, \\"Portals to Forever\\" is over 16 mins. And that's just two songs -- there's seven tracks, most of them long, giving time for the musicians to stretch out nicely with memorable solos. With more than 80 mins of music, most of it outstanding, it's going to give you what you hope for.<br /><br />So shield your eyes from the cover long enough to buy it. Then close your eyes and enjoy it.ane effectively in its intro and outro. But there were a couple of aspects of the book that I personally did not enjoy.<br /><br />First of all, I'm finding I agree with Orson Scott Card's theory on writing, using the \\"American Plain Style.\\" In his words that means, \\"the author tries to become as invisible as possible, bringing the reader to see things as if experiencing them along with the character, instead of having a writer constantly commenting and interrupting the flow of the story.\\" This book takes the opposite of that approach. It's just that most writers really make you feel like it's a climax, maybe draw out the action and meaning of what's going on. For him the last few pages and chapters move at an accelerated pace, one that I wish he'd maintained throughout the book. In that sense it's kind of the reverse of how it should be. And added that it's impossible to mutter an \\"eh\\" when finished with it, saying the book will change your life for the better.<br /><br />I think he's right about it changing things.<br /><br />The first 15 pages stormed me. 1400067820|3.0|The central insight of this book is important and deserves more attention. Unfortunately, reading it feels a little bit like being buttonholed at a party by someone you initially took to be charming and interesting, but turns out to be an insufferable, obsessive and self-absorbed windbag. Take heart! If you stick it out to the end, you may end up being amused, if not charmed, by Taleb's rambling rant. I was at least.<br /><br />The main idea (really a riff on his earlier two books) is that many systems - in e.g., nature, economics, social life, and politics - can actually benefit from (or be made stronger by) exposure to stress and/or volatility. Attempts to enforce stability and protect the system from stressors often are counterproductive and lead to even greater harm - when the big blow-up, the Black Swan event, eventually occurs. So very often, the right prescription (whether in medicine, economics or other areas where modern man is prone to intervene or \\"do something\\") is actually to do nothing at all. Not only will the patient recover, but will likely be stronger in the long run.<br /><br />Good advice, and the imperative for policy-makers to \\"do something\\" is certainly strong (maybe overwhelming) in today's political world. But one gets the feeling that Taleb is a crank, given how far he takes the theme - almost rejecting modernity itself in the process. His ranting against banks and finance and corporations (especially ones that sell us evil things like tobacco and soft drinks) begins to seem anti-capitalist, despite his repeated pleadings in favor of free markets and against statism. (I guess he thinks we should have simply banned tobacco, possibly Coke and Pepsi as well, but then would he trust the government with the responsibility? Certainly not the central government of a \\"nation state\\"! Maybe Mike Bloomberg?) In his section on the ethical consequences of his anti-fragility framework he seems almost socialistic, or at least in denial that the individualshould be at the center of ethics (hey, man, we're just here to pass our genes along, not to focus on \\"happiness\\" in our own lifetimes, right?). And he uses the anti-fragility theme to pedal some idiosyncratic (if not bizarre) ideas about exercise, medicine, diet and even the use of sunscreen (which he comes down against).<br /><br />Along the way we get to endure the list of things and people that Taleb deplores: financial models, professors (especially economics and business school professors), bankers, accountants, Thomas Friedman - it's a long list, and at times his antipathy is almost Krugman-like. He should lighten up - most employees of big companies are just trying to live their lives by their best lights, and their \\"middle class values\\" may be just okay.<br /><br />The biggest beef I have with this book in the end is, even if you accept Taleb's description of the problem, what's to be done? Nothing in here to point the way other than more people seeing the world as Taleb sees it. In the last section, for instance, he talks about the agency problem in big corporations and well-known problems of the motivations of politicians and \\"public servants\\". I see no incremental value to viewing these problems through Taleb's lens of anti-fragility. Public choice economics and corporate finance theory (I know! He hates economic and finance theory!!) identified these pathologies in the 1960's and `70's. The issues are not novel; the problem is how to fix the institutions to address the bad incentives.<br /><br />But if you don't mind spending the whole party with the smartest guy in the room (no one would be quicker to identify himself as such than Taleb himself - just imagine all the books he reads (and carries with him on airplanes - no e-books for him) and his ability to quote the ancients on every subject!), then you might get a laugh or two out of this book. And it just might make you think about the world a little differently. I guess that's worth the effort.

It's a haunting, lyrical, spell-binding tale, full of fluid prose and sharp insight into the human experience.<br /><br />Gaiman has a unique ability to meld reality and the amorphous, dream-like states we sometimes find ourselves in.<br /><br />In this story about a man who returns to the farm of his childhood friend and relives the nightmarish events of a time in his youth, Gaiman brings out the tenuous nature of memories, especially childhood memories. And because this is a fantasy piece, a lot of the memories seem unreal, and yet Gaiman infuses them with enough detail (a boy eating porridge at a kitchen table, a kitten's comforting purr against the cheek) to make it believable.<br /><br />The prose is as fluid as the story, effortlessly gliding between the ephemeral and the concrete: &#34;... the peculiar crinkling of space on space into dimensions that fold like origami and blossom like strange orchids.&#34; And every once in a while, he will slam the reader against a jagged truth that makes you pause: &#34;She was the storm, she was the lightening, she was the adult world with all its power and all its secrets and all its foolish casual cruelty.&#34;<br /><br />It's a very good story. And if there's any criticism to give, it's that the ending is - perhaps - not as resolute as some would like. But then again, the entire tale exists in a kind of dream realm, where things have no beginning and no end.<br /><br />So maybe the end is perfect.

This isn't a light read for a day at the beach or a brief plane ride, prepare to sacrifice a great deal of time and effort if you're considering reading this series and getting the most out of the experience. If you weren't a fan of the amount of commitment required from the previous book, then you should have no interest in its sequel. As for the actual quality of the book itself, George R. R. Martin has proven once again that he is among the highest caliber of fantasy writers, crafting yet another epic tale with such an astonishing sense of scope and memorable characters that it's once again apparent why this series continues to galvanize both readers and television viewers alike. However, there are quite a few issues that detract from Clash of Kings' overall gravitas. It succeeds as a worthy successor to the masterful Game of Thrones, yet it doesn't quite reach the same heights as its predecessor.<br /><br />With a book of such an enormous magnitude, it can be quite daunting to get into, let alone write a review that successfully does it justice on all accounts. I'll start with my favorite aspect of the series: the incredible world-building. As I said, George R. R. Martin is among the absolute apex of fantasy writers. The amount of detail and mythology he interweaves into his fiction is truly stunning; made even more impressive by the amount of believability he instills within the reader as they sift through the pages. There are slight undertones of magic and mythical beasts such as dragons and white walkers, yet the more fantastical elements never overshadow the amount of believability Martin has built the series' foundations around. It's a world that feels truly alive and absolutely gargantuan in comparison to other works in the genre. With the ever-changing narrative constantly shifting between the cast of characters, the reader is continuously treated to a plethora of settings. With each new location working to further immerse the reader into the fiction, and make the world pop out of the pages. From the searing deserts of Qarth, to the frozen tundra just beyond the Great Wall; the scope is simply unmatched. The icing on the cake is the believable, and very authentic medieval dialogue that permeates the series. Each of these elements contributes significantly into making the land of Westeros one of the most well-realized and iconic fantasy realms ever conceived by one man's brilliant imagination. Say goodbye to real-life for a while.<br /><br />Many aspects of the previous novel carry over into Clash of Kings: political intrigue, multiple character arcs, and the series' morally ambiguous tone. However the context of the story differs substantially. Whereas Game of Thrones was akin to a political thriller, Clash of Kings plays out more along the lines of a medieval war story; complete with all the backroom deals and cloak and dagger subterfuge that has come to be expected from the genre. Fortunately Martin conveys his tale in such a brilliant manner that it avoids becoming just another generic fantasy romp. An unfortunate downside to this however is the lack of twists to spice up the narrative. Make no mistake, there are a decent amount of plot elements and sudden revelations that'll keep you engaged, yet it lacks the same degree of unforeseen plot twists that its predecessor had. The result is a highly enjoyable, albeit a much more linear story.<br /><br />Yet it's the memorable cast of characters that is arguably the series' strongest attribute. In my opinion, this is where I become a bit conflicted on the series and why I can't give this book a five star rating. This book is chocked full of so many characters that I cannot possibly summarize my thoughts on all of them, so I will instead give my thoughts on the most predominant ones and their lasting impact on me. The two characters which Martin wrote brilliantly were the twin mothers of the story: Cersei Lannister and Catalyn Stark. Catalyn's personal grief towards her separated family was perhaps the story's greatest emotional highpoint; whereas in contrast, Cersei Lannister is devilishly cruel and manipulative in her pursuit to secure her family the Iron Throne. Where her character truly shines is through her own reluctance of being born a woman in a predominantly patriarchal society. She's easily one of my favorite villains in recent memory. Like the majority of the characters in Clash of Kings, everyone shows human signs of triumph and defeat in their mad dash for power, or their pursuit of salvation in the eye of the storm. No character is without their flaws and nobody is walking away from this conflict without some scars.<br /><br />On the other end of the spectrum are the characters that unfortunately didn't click with me in one manner or another. Keep in mind that for every character I dislike, there were at least two more I loved in this book. I'm certain to garner a large amount of flak for this, but I personally don't enjoy the story arc of Jon Snow. With so many character arcs intertwined in the civil war for the Iron Throne and those simply trying to survive the chaos.  It significantly hurts the pacing and overall sense of urgency of the narrative when the reader is seemingly ripped out of the moment and forced into a conflict that has seemingly no effect on the rest of the book. Jon's ordeal beyond the Great Wall feels completely isolated and irrelevant to the larger scheme of events that transpire. I'm sure he'll become more relevant in the later novels, yet as for now, I find his character underwhelming and his arc bland in comparison to the others. Contrast this with Daenerys Targaryen, another character who is largely isolated from the main events of the book. Unlike Jon Snow, Daenerys doesn't suffer from the same issues that plague Jon's chapters. Daenerys is an upcoming monarch who will undoubtedly have a massive impact on the events transpiring in the series. Like her dragons, she emits a greater presence that threatens to shake the foundations of the world. In other words, she feels integral to the story despite being isolated from the main events.<br /><br />Yet the character that drags the story down the most is unfortunately Bran Stark, a character that has completely overstayed his welcome. He succeeded in eliciting sympathy in the first book, yet now he's nothing more than a dead-weight that completely brings the narrative to a halt. Throughout the entire one thousand plus pages, Bran does nothing but whine about his broken legs while basking in his own dreams of transhumanism. It was saddening to see him suffer in the first book, yet he goes through little to no development in Clash of Kings, instead repeating the same morose thoughts that we've already seen far too many times in the previous book. It's hard to care really when the entire kingdom is engulfed in civil war with people dying by the hundreds. His broken legs feel insignificant in comparison. Which is why I was so surprised that Rob Stark wasn't given his own story arc. Unlike Bran, Rob has a massive impact on the story and actual development to undergo. In a book based entirely around the conflict of warring monarchs, it's truly bizarre that Martin hasn't done the character justice by providing him with his own chapters. I find the premise of his character far more compelling: a young boy forced into the horrors of war and the burden of leadership. All the wasted time moping with Bran could have been productively used on Rob instead.<br /><br />My final issue is one that even the first book suffered from, albeit to a much lesser extent. This series has far too many minor characters than Martin knows what to do with. Most of them end up feeling like a list of names rather than actual characters that have any real effect on the story or possess any semblance of characterization. Trying to remember every minor character in each story arc can be quite a taxing challenge for the reader, again this isn't a book for casual readers. This problem also diminishes the strong theme of family that was so endearing in the previous book. It's not completely absent, yet the companionship found within the Stark family felt like the heart of Game of Thrones. It gave the first book a great deal of character and further invested the reader in the combined fates of the characters. I'm hoping to see more interaction between the major characters in the next book. The minor characters simply don't offer much to care about.<br /><br />Make no mistake; Clash of Kings is a brilliant novel that die-hard fans of fantasy will devour. It isn't without its faults, being some characters feel underwhelming and the overuse of minor characters is a little trying at times. Yet the positives easily outweigh the negatives; the story and scale are both truly epic, the world feels alive and believable, and the tone is morally ambiguous and undeniably intoxicating. Here's to hoping my issues are remedied in the sequel.

I think this is one of those albums that will grow on you the more you listen to it, so I recommend it for any fan of groove metal or just metal ingeneral. B000BMYWYC|5.0|Bought this at a local hobby shop for about 6.00 bucks or so.<br /><br />The product is great. I like the thickness and shape of the glass bottle, and the ridges on the cap. The brush nearly reaches the bottom of the bottle (Which is kind of redundant - Unless you're using this as a primary modeling glue for American/non-snap fit kits, then this stuff will last you a VERY long time.).<br /><br />The cement itself is great. When applied to most model-kit plastics, it will slightly 'melt' the outer layer of plastic, which allows you to cleanly press and fuse parts together. This is wonderful for Gundam parts that need fixing. Most glues create an outer 'shell' of glue (and it usually looks nasty) that doesn't hold objects together... this stuff literally fuses a part together, making it near impossible for it to separate unless you repeat the reason why the part was broken in the first place! :p Haha.<br /><br />All in all, 5 stars. Great super-thin cement, easy to apply to odd and small areas, fuses parts for a near perfect hold, and will last at least a few months to a couple years. I've had mine 6 months already, and I'm not even a quarter way through the bottle. And NO evaporation! :D

One of his favorite instruments outside of the brass and woodwind sections is the piano...and grand pianos have 88 keys! That's one key for every year he's been on this earth...I don't think I'll ever look at another grand piano and not think of him as long as I live! In the Applications folder on the Mac, Google Chrome shows-up alphabetically under G for &#34;Google Chrome&#34;. And they just don't look good on a bigger TV.  The lower resolution videos, you almost have to watch on a smaller window or device, so that you can even make out what's really going on.  Both my TVs already are smart TVs with a YouTube app.  But because of navigating an on-screen keyboard on the TV with a remote, the Chromecast method of watching a YouTube video, barely one-ups this.  One problem, is that when I was watching a video on YouTube via Chromecast, I wanted to change to another video, in the middle of  the previous video still playing.  When I did this, the newly chosen video just wouldn't play.  I got stuck with a gear turning and nothing happening.  This seems to happen less on my iPad than on my iPhone.  But it's pretty frequent.  Hmm, is this Beta too ?<br /><br />  Next I tried to cast a tab from Google Chrome on my computer, to my TV.  At first the, video is jagged and elements of the webpage were distorted.  But this smoothed-out after alottle bit.  The text (even very large text on a webpage) looked crappy.  I'm the only person on my wireless network, sitting 6 feet from my wireless router, which my TV is hardwired to.  And there is a very significant lag & time delay of displaying webpages.  For no explainable reason, sometimes the lag was much more significant than at other times.  Again, with me being the only person on the network.  Playing videos on CNN.com, from a Google Chrome Tab to the TV, was kind of cool in concept.  But, the audio was way, way off.  Also, the video was jerky.  And I have 50 down, 25 up Verizon Fios. I don't think internet connection is an issue here.  Also, casting a tab happens locally over the wi-fi network.  I discovered this same video played without being Chromecasted, with no delayed audio and without the jerkiness.  Maybe we can blame this on the fact that, casting a tab from Chrome is.... You guessed it - BETA !<br /><br />  So what is this Chromecast thing good for ?  Maybe the occasional YouTube video.  Maybe if you just had to show something from the internet on the TV, although not reliably.  For Netflix, I was already a Netflix subscriber and have 2 iPhones, an iPad, a Roku player, and two newer Samsung smart TVs. Would be the only reason.  And the YouTube thing... It's really a novelty.  In reality, alot of us are watching youtube videos on YouTube's website maybe not in Chrome, or watching a YouTube video that someone posted on FaceBook.  And those videos won't play on Chromecast.  Only videos you watch in the YouTube App will go to Chromecast.  That is weak.  And you can't cast a tab from your iPad even if you're using Chrome. This thing is cool for a dongle, yet weak for a set-top box type device.  I'd say Google Chromecast is a device that is not ready for primetime.  It's not clear what it's good for at this moment.  But it definitely doesn't do what it's supposed to do, very well.  But it does it.  Maybe it's a poor-man's AppleTV... But isn't that the position, the Roku LT takes @ $49 ?!  They can't really be compared though.  Because this thing is an HDMI dongle.  I'm not sure where the Chromecast fits-in between streaming media players and smart TVs etc.  The Chromecast might be good for that person who refuses to pay for content or buy a Roku & AppleTV.  The person that watches video from a computer/websites and wants it on their TV.  Maybe it's for the PC user - Who thinks bad behavior like this is normal and doesn't mind the jerkiness or out-of-sync audio (Haha, I'm a Mac guy).  It might be good for someone who doesn't have a Smart TV.  This is great for the people out there, that don't know the AppleTV or Roku boxes exist. But even though the AppleTV is referred to as Apple's &#34;hobby&#34; product... It still works better than Google's (Beta) Chromecast.  If you're that guy that thinks using a computer is going to sit down at your desk with the monster tower computer, and you're looking for a way to watch the crap you download off torrents etc., that you want to see on your big TV.... Then this is for you.  Maybe if you're that guy that complains how Macs are too expensive and that a PC cost 1/3rd the price, then this, at $35 (versus AppleTV's $99), is for you. 140006922X|5.0|One of best books I've ever read on bringing clearer insight into who Jesus was and what his teachings and mission were about.<br /><br />This account of Jesus of Nazareth and his followers is the most logical I have ever read. While the author freely admits there is a shortage of historical information on Jesus of Nazareth's life, he gathers more than enough tangential information to accurately paint the historical environment and situation that Jesus operated within.<br /><br />Yes, this book is polarizing, as evidenced by all the 1-star reviews claiming the author is either unqualified or inept. It's just the usual response to a book on either religion and politics: where logic and common sense have little bearing on the emotions of engrained superstitions. And yes, religion, any religion, is a superstition, which is nothing more than \\"belief without evidence.\\"<br /><br />But, in my opinion, the most poignant facts provided in this book are contained in the last 4-6 paragraphs:<br /><br />  \\"Paul's conception of Christianity may have been anathema before 70 C.E. But afterward, his notion of a wholly new religion free from the authority of a Temple that no longer existed, unburdened by a law that no longer mattered, and divorced from a Judaism that had become a pariah was enthusiastically embraced by converts throughout the Roman Empire. Hence, in 398 C.E., when, according to legend, another group of bishops gathered at a council in the city of Hippo Regius in modern-day Algeria to canonize what would become known as the New Testament, they chose to include in the Christian scriptures one letter from James, the brother and successor of Jesus, two letters from Peter, the chief apostle and first among the Twelve, three letters from John, the beloved disciple and pillar of the church, and fourteen letters from Paul, the deviant and outcast who was rejected and scorned by the leaders in Jerusalem. In fact, more than half of the twenty-seven books that now make up theNew Testament are either by or about Paul.<br />  This should not be surprising. Christianity after the destruction of Jerusalem was almost exclusively a gentile religion; it needed a gentile theology. And that is precisely what Paul provided. The choice between James's vision of a Jewish religion anchored in the Law of Moses and derived from a Jewish nationalist who fought against Rome, and Paul's vision of a Roman religion that divorced itself from Jewish provincialism and required nothing for salvation save belief in Christ, was not a difficult one for the second and third generations of Jesus's followers to make.\\"<br /><br />As such, and based on the construction of the New Testament, the religion of Christianity is based on human creativity, interpretation and manipulation, and on a number of self-serving agendas, and not on the teaching and life of a man named Jesus of Nazareth. Why? Because the true religion of Jesus of Nazareth would have been based on the letters of James and Peter, and most definitely not Paul. Christianity is a man-made religion, just as is Judaism and Islam.<br /><br />Most humans have a need to believe that they exist for a reason other than just being part of God's creation, unwilling to base their happiness on fulfilling the role God intended, and thus they have the need to believe in an afterlife, which is the \\"drug\\" most religions feed to their congregation. There is nothing more \\"human\\" than a driving need to believe in a human's self-importance and thus need to have a life hereafter. This book contradicts that drug, and just as a junkie will do anything to get another fix, so too will all the superstitionists berate this book.<br /><br />If you have an open mind, then you should read this book. And then you can continue to believe your superstitions, but with possibly a little more knowledge of how all those superstitions came into being and how the mission of Jesus of Nazareth got manipulated into something it was never intendedto be.

B00CW7KK9K|5.0|My feeling is that a lot of people think of this device as something it isn't. In my opinion, the Withings Pulse is not a pedometer. It is an activity tracking device with the added benefit of a highly accurate pulse rate detector as well as a basic sleep quality monitor.<br /><br />The real value of the Pulse is in the Withings Health Mate reporting website. It has been reported that distance measured and actual steps taken may not be accurate and this may likely be true but it's also relative. What is important in my mind is the trend over time. What difference does it make if you know you trekked 9500 steps and the device reports 10500 steps if your long term average is only 2000 steps per day.<br /><br />Unfortunately, at present, the Health Mate website doesn't show an accumulated history of steps taken as a graph over time but Withings has a track record of constantly evolving. I predict that there will be continual innovation with this device as users report their experiences and suggestions for additional features.<br /><br />A couple of caveats: Setup is not all that intuitive. The getting started guide is not detailed enough. I was able to activate and pair my device but had the feeling that I solved a Rubik's cube after completion. Regardless,I have not had one problem in the two weeks since with every day usage. One suggestion that I have is to charge the device for 8 hours once received. You will have to resist the temptation to tinker with it but you will have better success in pairing with a fully charged unit.<br /><br />For persons who want even more information regarding usage, there is a Facebook page which is constantly monitored by a representative of Withings. They seem to be extremely responsive which further tells me that this device has good support and most likely a bright future ahead of it.<br /><br />So if you think of this device as a long term activity tracker and not simply an immediate monitor of your 10K run, I think you will be more than satisfied especially as the statistical reporting improves and evolves over time.<br /><br />I am genuinely impressed with the features of the Withings Pulse and I believe that they have executed their vision well. I never leave home without it.

I had to listen to it a few times before I could see it 'clear the fence'.  Tempest, Roll On John, and Narrow Way are just three from this collection that show Dylan's diverse writing style, his bands' superb chops, and his great phrasing of the lyrics despite an increasingly gravelly voice.<br /><br />With anyone else telling the story in Tempest, it would be a throwaway. 1451695195|3.0|After reading PROOF of HEAVEN, I honestly wanted to give full credence to Eben Alexander's supposed encounter with the afterlife. His descriptions of this experience are detailed but still believably vague and quite attractive in many respects. I also like the fact that Dr. Alexander does not attempt to validate (or invalidate) any one organized religion or creed according to his NDE. Prior to becoming ill and having these experiences, Alexander had been a fairly undecided, disinterested, liberal Christian; yet, interesting enough, he often refers to the God that he apparently encountered as \\"Om,\\" which is, of course, a basic phoneme/pronoun in Hindu-Buddhist mantra.<br /><br />Much hinges on Alexander's claim that, due to the fact that he was in a coma induced by E. Coli Meningitis with his cerebral cortex completely shut down, his experience is more valid than most other NDEs:  \\".  .  .I was allowed to die harder, and travel deeper, than almost all NDE subjects before me. As arrogant as that may sound, my intentions are not\\"(78).<br /><br />After reading portions of \\"The Prophet,\\" an article Luke Dittrich published in ESQUIRE {August, 2013}, I STILL want to believe Dr. Alexander's account but feel compelled to take it with a healthy dose of salt. Most importantly, Dittrich refutes Alexander's fundamental claim that he was completely comatose, quoting one of Alexander's attending physicians--Dr. Laura Potter, who Alexander makes frequent reference to in his book--as stating that the alleged \\"coma\\" was medically induced and the author's cerebral cortex was NOT, as he claims, in a state of total shut-down. Thus, Alexander's experiences could easily be due to simple hallucination. PROOF of HEAVEN, for the record, contains a brief \\"Statement\\" at the end by Dr. Scott Wade, another attending physician who verifies Alexander's claim that he was indeed in a completely comatose, vegetative state. Alexander describes Wade, btw, as \\"a friend and a neighbor\\" (105).<br /><br />And who's to say? It's possible that Dittrich's article is full of disinformation. Given the serious allegations Dittrich makes, however, it's hard to see what he could gain from trying to discredit Alexander unduly or what motivation he would have for lying about the author and his book. Along with Dr. Potter's statements, Dittrich alleges (with documentation) that Alexander has been subject to several serious malpractice suits, has lied in an attempt to cover up his malpractices, and, perhaps most glaringly, has been fired by several hospitals and prohibited from performing surgery due to his past blunders. That in itself does not disprove PROOF of HEAVEN; nevertheless, seeing the way Alexander touts his credentials as a neurosurgeon without mentioning any of these factors, it does raise questions about his essential credibility.<br /><br />Like many others, I have a serious interest in NDE studies and yearn to know more about what/whatnot really happens when we die. I could go on and on regarding PROOF of HEAVEN's more attractive and less credible qualities. In the end, though, if any of us want to have any hope of learning anything about this enormous question, we have to look at all the accounts rationally, laying aside the tendency to simply go with what sounds good to us. And that's the toughest part of it all.<br /><br />Some other, perhaps more reliable, books I would recommend (all three are also by M.D.'s): [[ASIN:0062517392 Life After Life: The Investigation of a Phenomenon--Survival of Bodily Death]], or anything else by Dr. Moody, [[ASIN:0061452572 Evidence of the Afterlife: The Science of Near-Death Experiences]], and [[ASIN:0307731715 To Heaven and Back: A Doctor's Extraordinary Account of Her Death, Heaven, Angels, and Life Again: A True Story]].And who's to say? B004EPYZT4|3.0|Given the legendary status of the 1982 classic of the same name, watching this re-make without any comparison with original proved to be impossible. So I watched it twice, the second time purely to digest this film on its own merit. Overall, I believe that it was a respectable attempt to capture the magic and sweeping majesty of the 1982 version, and that the cast took this enterprise seriously, but it was not a great film. The success of the original was, to a large degree, due to the creative team and the cast's ability to transport the audience to a different world, a world of high adventure prior to recorded history, and kept them there for the duration of that film. In this regard, Arnold's limited acting ability, strange (at the time) accent, equally strong accents by some of the other cast members, and stilted dialogue did not hurt the effort; in fact, these factors actually enhanced the film by presenting to the audience a sense of exoticism that people could rightfully expect from a mythical world tens of thousands of years ago. Arnold's rather thin acting skills actually gave his cinematic interpretation of Conan a sense of bombastic grandiosity, which is why Conan is still one of Arnold's most celebrated roles. The original film was also balanced very well on a character and a thematic basis, which is a sign of the creative depth of the original team. On Conan's side: Conan's indomitable masculinity was balanced by Valeria's raw physical femininity; Conan's implacable strength was balanced by Subotai's wit, speed, and deadly accuracy. Thematically: Conan's physical power and strength of will were balanced by Thulsa Doom's towering charisma and insidious intellect. The entire cast was giving enough material to be memorable, especially the bad guys. The portrayal of Thulsa Doom by the incomparable James Earl Jones created one of the most well-known villains in cinematic history. Even his henchmen were memorable: who doesn't remember the hulking bearded blade master who was finally bested by Conan and Valeria, or the Hammer Guy? Despite their relatively few scenes, their treatment in the film gave even minor characters enough credibility as powerful bad guys not to be taken lightly in the context of the film. The music, despite the times, is still celebrated as a master piece.<br />For the new film, from the poster and still shots, it would appear that the film makers captured the Conan from the books: imposing, dark, and brooding. The stills of the veteran actor Stephen Lang as the antagonist Khalar Zym were also impressive. From what I can see, serious money was spent on creating sets and visual effects for the world of Conan. However, it was the delivery that was off. The speech and mannerisms of the cast all came off as too modern, too familiar, and in some cases, too slick and polished. So despite that the visual aspects of the film were attempting the transport the audience to a different world of fantasy and adventure, the cast's acting and speech kept bring the audience back to the here and now. This is especially disappointing given how successfully Jason Mamoa portrayed an ancient warrior on the small screen in The Game of Thrones. Also, other than Conan and Khalar Zym, none of the other characters were given enough material or treatment in the film to be particularly noteworthy in the context of the film. They could be easily dismissed as \\"henchmen 1, 2, 3\\" \\"damsel in distress,\\" \\"happy pirate buddy,\\" etc. It's not a very engaging film that can grab and retain the audience's undivided attention from the beginning to the end. In this regard, I don't blame the actors, they are all decent to good actors for the most part, the script, directing, and editing have much to do with the success of a film, and these elements did not mesh very well for this one.

0486284735|5.0|Wrote this review for the Livermore Public Library 2013 Summer Reading Program:<br /><br />Movie director Joe Wright described her as heroic.<br /><br />Oh, no, not Lizzy Bennett. He admired Lizzy's ridiculous mother, Mrs. Bennett who's entire purpose for living is to see her daughters married. Mrs. Bennett goes to great lengths to get this job done and provides the lion's share of the comic relief in Pride and Prejudice.<br /><br />It is the story of Elizabeth Bennett, of course, and her affection for her sister Jane, who hopes to be soon engaged to Mr. Bingly. Jane Austen's masterpiece has been the model for the Romance genre since it was published in 1813. The language may be dated, but it is a surprisingly easy read for a classic, and it's light tone makes it something of a pleasure to read.<br /><br />Austen breaks a lot of modern rules for writing fiction, telling what is before showing it stands at the forefront. However, her style tends to make circumstances and characters far easier to understand for modern readers. At the core of the book are the way Elizabeth Bennett and Fitzwilliam Darcy relate to each other.<br /><br />Darcy's absence from most of the text, however, gives him a ghost like quality, as if he is only haunting the minds of those thinking of him. And those thinking of him seem to universally despise him. Why is it that Lizzy Bennett cannot stop thinking of this loathsome man? Into this intrigue land a pair of suitors who have their own faults that Lizzy must discover, overcoming her own prejudices that colour her view of the men in her life.<br /><br />Pride and Prejudice is about misleading perception and how the world we live in may be very different from the world we see. As simple a read as it is, it is a complex story with a simple heart, and more than a few laughs. It would win no awards for style with modern editors, but it is still a masterpiece and well worth reading.

0743297334|3.0|Wrote this review for the Livermore Public Library 2013 Summer Reading Program:<br /><br />Robert Cohn is a sober, former College Boxing Champion supposedly working on a writing career in Paris. Robert Cohn is the only sober character in The Sun Also Rises, alcohol flows freely and the characters seem to eat it for sustenance whether they're drinking Martinis in Paris, or Rioja for the Running of the Bulls in Pomplona.<br /><br />What Robert Cohn has in common with his friend Jake Barnes is his affection for a Lady. The genuinely titled Lady Ashley has taken up with a very bankrupt lush named Michael, who hates Robert, loves Jake and is the fourth in this complex relationship between these four people.<br /><br />Hemingway's economy of words is on full display in this novel, and I could not imagine trying to speed read this book, as every word counts. Part of the problem with The Sun Also Rises is that it is written for a contemporary readership, so Hemingway takes for granted that much of how life is lived in 1926 is common knowledge.<br /><br />It is the responsibility of the reader to understand some of the things he mentioned. Fortunately, these items are few. I would recommend watching a good movie that features a similar setting and dialogue before diving into this novel. It is short enough to read twice in a week, so one may grasp its finer points, and to, perhaps, do a little research on the period

0684803356|5.0|Wrote this review for the Livermore Public Library 2013 Summer Reading Program:<br /><br />Blow up a bridge. That is the mission assigned to Robert Jordan.<br /><br />Blow it up at at the perfect time for it to be blown up, and that time is not set... he must know when to do it, and he has to figure that out himself.<br /><br />This is how the story of Robert Jordan, an American Expatriot opens, in the midst of the backdrop of the Spanish Civil War. Hemingway does a great job setting the scene, describing just enough detail to put you there. He also manages to give you a nice taste of Spanish culture, and it's various attitudes.<br /><br />The Spaniard's love of seafood is featured in Pilar's description of Valencia, Spain is second only to Japan in seafood consumption.<br /><br />What is very interesting is the dialogue. Hemingway goes to great lengths to accurately translate the way Spaniards speak to each other with formality. There are a lot of Thee's and Thys, and the structure of the speech is a little difficult to get used to.<br /><br />It took me a while to understand why I kept reading things like \\"I have indescribable and unprintable and I have forgotten the obscenity password.\\" Indescribable, unprintable, and obscenity probably have more to do with the censors of 1940 than anything else. Still, it does a nice job of conveying the notion that an obscenity in Spanish carries a lot more weight than does one in English.<br /><br />This novel is high adventure, and Robert Jordan, his political affiliations notwithstanding, is a hero's hero. If you decide to look into reading a book that sits among the list of classics, here's one that truly deserves the title!

B008JFUO4U|4.0|I'll make this short and sweet :- the similarities in plot devices and parallels between Oblivion and Moon (2009) struck me immediately. They are not identical, but their premises are oblique - which is not meant as criticism as much as its meant as comparison. Where one features a presumed man isolated on a mining base on the moon, the other posits a presumed man on earth following an alien invasion. Both have memories of people and situations in the past which they can't quite identify. Both come to confront replicas of their own physical beings. Both discover that they are androids. Both \\"sets\\" of androids die. Etc. Their are other, rather fundamental differences. Oblivion's Hans Zimmer-like score is maddening and distracting in the extreme. As usual, where this kind of thing is concerned, the minimalism in the music should not be the pounding, ostinato-driven repetition - but the MINIMAL use of music so as to ENHANCE its effect when its presence is required. When the score is nothing but another layer of unchanging sound, it ceases to be music and becomes noise -  it destroys, utterly, the purpose and real magic of music. Moon is much more recondite in its use of music. All this said, the best comparison of the two films would be by way of musical simile: where Moon is like chamber music (small in scope - restricted in range), Oblivion is operatic. Both are fine in their own ways - and, my objections to the score for Oblivion notwithstanding, both films work in their own, distinctive ways.

140006922X|5.0|It's not often I find a book which ends with a sentence so powerful I had to put the book down.  It's not often I read a book, indie or not, that leaves me so compelled to leave a review, that I spend two full days trying to compose exactly what I want to say in my head.<br /><br />The book is a controversial one, I'll give you that.  It presents and idea that Western theology rails against so often.  What was the historical Jesus really like?<br /><br />I'll preface this review by letting you know, I am a studying theologian.  I currently possess a BA in theology (Medieval Theology with an emphasis in the compilation of the Canon), so I picked this book up with bias.<br /><br />I was first introduced to Reza Aslan in a Fox News interview in which he spent the entire time defending himself against a woman who could not move past the fact that he was a Muslim writing a book about Jesus.  I will just say I sincerely hope she watches that interview and drowns herself in shame.  Reza Aslan has the education I crave, and that alone intrigued me about his writing.<br /><br />I was a little nervous, picking up the book.  It wasn't expensive for the hard cover, which I was really excited to see on amazon.  Didn't hurt the wallet, which is always a huge plus for a mother of three school-aged children.<br /><br />I'm not sure if many of the readers in this blog spend a lot of time reading theology books, or non-fiction theory books, so in case you are unfamiliar with the general lay-out of these types of reads-- they can be very dry.  Dry to the point of wanting to throw the book across the room and never pick it up again.  While I was at University so many of the books I was forced to read made me want to cry tears of blood.<br /><br />Luckily Azlan has a real talent for story telling.  I was captivated from page one.  From his short introduction which introduced the reader to himself, his education, and more importantly, his religious background.  He was a convert to evangelical Christianity until (like a lot of theology students I know) he began to study religion.  Like me, the knowledge he amassed removed the mysticism and fear from the religion and the ability to believe went with it.<br /><br />That doesn't meant there isn't a story there, or there isn't a very important history.  I'll quote from one of the last passages in the book, \\"...Jesus the man--is every bit as compelling, charismatic, and praiseworthy as Jesus the Christ.  He is, in short, someone worth believing in.\\"<br /><br />The truth is, and most theologians-- whether they are religious or secular-- will tell you the Bible is not a historical document.  The New Testament's first scriptures ever written (the epistles) were in a time when there was a conflict between the Greek-educated Jews, and those left behind to spread the word of Jesus.  The first Gospels written (Mark, Matthew, then Luke followed by John) in a time after Jerusalem had been razed, burnt to the ground, and the Jews forced out.  The Gospels are a political tract, in short, to draw attention to why the Christians were okay, because they were so, very different from the Religion they had spawned from.<br /><br />But therein lies the problem-- there is little to no history about Jesus the man, a poor wood worker, raised in a providence that was steeped in revolution and oppression.<br /><br />Aslan takes information about the area where Jesus grew up, about the people history has recorded, and the politics at the time, and begins to craft a well-told story about what Jesus of Nazareth may have truly been like.<br /><br />The book is theory, there's no denying that.  There is no written evidence left aside from a few written words by Josephus, that mentions Jesus in a historical context.  The Q document is only theory, and even that was dated after the death of the Preacher.<br /><br />I'll say it again, it's not often I am this captivated by a theology book.  Theology is my favorite thing to read, but very rarely do I feel a sense of loss, a craving for more, when the book has ended.<br /><br />This is a must read for those of you even remotely curious about the history regarding one of the most charismatic and flourishing religions in known history.  Many times I've asked myself, \\"But how?  How did the words of Paul become so popular, and those of the men who walked with Jesus come to be almost completely forgotten in the religious dogma?\\"<br /><br />This book answers that question, and it's such a simple answer, I couldn't believe I hadn't thought about it before.  In all my studies, in all of my delving into how the canon was complied into the Bible we know today, how did I not, for a moment, put it all together.<br /><br />I won't give you the answer, but the moment you see it, you'll understand it, too.<br /><br />This is a completely unorthodox book review, but I don't care.  This book deserves the notoriety it's gained, and I'm happy to give it five stars.ery rarely do I feel a sense of loss, a craving for more, when the book has ended.<br /><br />This is a must read for those of you even remotely curious about the history regarding one of the most charismatic and flourishing religions in known history.  Many times I've asked myself, \\"But how?  How did the words of Paul become so popular, and those of the men who walked with Jesus come to be almost completely forgotten in the religious dogma?\\"<br /><br />This book answers that question, and it's such a simple answer, I couldn't believe I hadn't thought about it before.  In all my studies, in all of my delving into how the canon was complied into the Bible we know today, how did I not, for a moment, put it all together.<br /><br />I won't give you the answer, but the moment you see it, you'll understand it, too.<br /><br />This is a completely unorthodox book review, but I don't care.  This book deserves the notoriety it's gained, and I'm happy to give it five stars.

0143038419|5.0|I've read some of these reviews with great interest in the deep divide between those who loved this book and those who despised it.<br /><br />For those who despised it because they think the author is narcissistic, self-absorbed, and too focused on her own inner process than the magical places she visited - you've missed the whole point. This book is a spiritual memoir. It is *supposed* to be self-centered. While she travels to some exotic locales, the real journey is an internal one. The entire and stated point of her travels is self-exploration and self-healing. Of *course* she is going to talk about herself.<br /><br />For those who can't stand that the author is \\"privileged\\"... I just don't get it. Do you think wealth solves all problems? Do you think Gilbert's life is going to be sunshine and roses just because she can afford to travel? I don't even know how to respond to reviewers who think that simply the state of having money is enough to heal a deeply broken heart, and that being \\"privileged\\" somehow makes a woman's spiritual journey irrelevant.<br /><br />I really enjoy reading about people's spiritual lives and am grateful to those who have the courage to share them. Some of these experiences are very difficult to put into words, and I found Gilbert's descriptions to be poetic and compelling. Sharing this kind of deep personal change is a gift to those of us who identify as spiritual seekers.

0307730700|5.0|Fearless is the true story of Navy Seal Adam Brown. When this book arrived at my house via the Blogging for Books program graciously sponsored by Multnomah Books, I had no idea of the trauma that was ahead.<br /><br />Outside of my typical reading choices, reluctantly I plowed in. Almost from the beginning, Eric Blehm had me. Fast paced, brutally honest, and rippling with tension, the author does a marvelous job. In fact, Blehm does such a great job; I encountered emotions not recently touched. At one point, I threw the book down on my desk and sobbed. This crying continued for several days as I refused to pick the book back up. Not my typical response to reading a book on theology or some Christian living principle, which is my normal staple.<br /><br />From the onset, I knew that Adam died, but I simply did not want to get to that point in the story. As the story unfolds, a bonding takes place between the reader and Adam. Perhaps that is too limited. The relationship is between the reader and the entire group of players in the book would be more accurate.<br /><br />One cannot read this book and not fall in love with Adam's wife, his children, his peers and this hero. Adam is presented with all of his warts exposed, yet you end up cheering for him as he overcomes obstacles that would derail most humans. Adam simply did not know the word quit. Neither did his wife Kelley.<br /><br />I knew the date that Adam died. As the book sped along towards that event, I grew in my apprehension. It was coming, and I couldn't do anything about it. All the troubles, heartaches, struggles, failures and victories would end soon and my anger grew. My mind raced to seeing Kelley and the children collapsing when receiving the news. The funeral. The words shared by Adam's peers. All made it very difficult to finish this book. It simply is hard to read when you can't see due to crying.<br /><br />I would challenge any man to read this book and not be moved. Eric Blehm does an outstanding job of honoring those that truly deserve it. While I don't necessarily like to encounter such emotional stress, once I picked up the thrown book and finished it, in some strange way, I felt fearless.<br /><br />Dr. Jeff Klickg job of honoring those that truly deserve it. While I don't necessarily like to encounter such emotional stress, once I picked up the thrown book and finished it, in some strange way, I felt fearless.<br /><br />Dr. Jeff Klick
0375703764|4.0|After completing House of Leaves by Mark Danielewski, I know that I will one day read it again. I have not read much ergodic literature and its use is interesting and infuriating in equal parts. It's the type of novel where pages are read, and then reviewed again, with care and attention to detail. Whether it's the changing typeface, alignment, or the hundreds of footnotes, House of Leaves is not a book one simply reads in a few sittings.<br /><br />Out of a desire to not spoil the book for others, I will summarize gently. This is a multilayered novel that uses a collection of documents, interviews, and theories to weave together a postmodern tapestry. To some, the novel is a love story, to others it's horrific, and for those not yet classified, it's castes a jocular eye upon literary criticism.<br /><br />On certain pages I wanted to rate the novel five stars, on other pages I wanted to sweep it into my trash bin. However, after completing it, I rate it four stars. It is definitely worth reading, and I will one day pick it up again. On its own, the book is an accomplishment that is worthy of review by people who appreciate literature and different ways we can be absorbed by a good story.
